<span class="italic"><a title="Link to journal home page" href="https://doi.org/10.1039/2046-2069/2011">RSC Adv.</a></span>, 2018, <strong>8</strong>, 2477-2484</div><h1 id="sect213"><span class="title_heading">Three-dimensional macroporous graphene monoliths with entrapped MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> nanoflakes from single-step synthesis for high-performance sodium-ion batteries<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a></span></h1><p class="header_text">
<span class="bold">Linfeng Fei</span><span class="orcid"><a target="_blank" title="Select to open ORCID record for Linfeng&nbsp;Fei (orcid.org/0000-0001-7329-0019) in a new window" id="connect-orcid-link" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0001-7329-0019"><img id="orcid-id-logo" src="/content/NewImages/orcid_16x16.png" alt="ORCID logo"></a></span><sup><span class="sup_ref italic">a</span></sup>, 
<span class="bold">Ming Xu</span><sup><span class="sup_ref italic">ac</span></sup>, 
<span class="bold">Juan Jiang</span><sup><span class="sup_ref italic">ad</span></sup>, 
<span class="bold">Sheung Mei Ng</span><sup><span class="sup_ref italic">a</span></sup>, 
<span class="bold">Longlong Shu</span><sup><span class="sup_ref italic">b</span></sup>, 
<span class="bold">Li Sun</span><span class="orcid"><a target="_blank" title="Select to open ORCID record for Li&nbsp;Sun (orcid.org/0000-0002-2108-0797) in a new window" id="connect-orcid-link" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0002-2108-0797"><img id="orcid-id-logo" src="/content/NewImages/orcid_16x16.png" alt="ORCID logo"></a></span><sup><span class="sup_ref italic">e</span></sup>, 
<span class="bold">Keyu Xie</span><span class="orcid"><a target="_blank" title="Select to open ORCID record for Keyu&nbsp;Xie (orcid.org/0000-0001-7719-9095) in a new window" id="connect-orcid-link" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0001-7719-9095"><img id="orcid-id-logo" src="/content/NewImages/orcid_16x16.png" alt="ORCID logo"></a></span><sup><span class="sup_ref italic">fg</span></sup>, 
<span class="bold">Haitao Huang</span><span class="orcid"><a target="_blank" title="Select to open ORCID record for Haitao&nbsp;Huang (orcid.org/0000-0002-3861-2702) in a new window" id="connect-orcid-link" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0002-3861-2702"><img id="orcid-id-logo" src="/content/NewImages/orcid_16x16.png" alt="ORCID logo"></a></span><sup><span class="sup_ref italic">a</span></sup>, 
<span class="bold">Chi Wah Leung</span><sup><span class="sup_ref italic">a</span></sup>, 
<span class="bold">Chee Leung Mak</span>*<sup><span class="sup_ref italic">a</span></sup><span class="bold"> and </span>
<span class="bold">Yu Wang</span>*<sup><span class="sup_ref italic">b</span></sup>
<br><a id="affa"><sup><span class="sup_ref italic">a</span></sup></a><span class="italic">Department of Applied Physics, The Hong Kong Polytechnic University, Hong Kong SAR, China. E-mail: <a href="mailto:apaclmak@polyu.edu.hk">apaclmak@polyu.edu.hk</a></span>
<br><a id="affb"><sup><span class="sup_ref italic">b</span></sup></a><span class="italic">School of Materials Science and Engineering, Nanchang University, Nanchang, Jiangxi 330031, China. E-mail: <a href="mailto:wangyu@ncu.edu.cn">wangyu@ncu.edu.cn</a></span>
<br><a id="affc"><sup><span class="sup_ref italic">c</span></sup></a><span class="italic">School of Metallurgical and Environment, Central South University, Changsha 410083, China</span>
<br><a id="affd"><sup><span class="sup_ref italic">d</span></sup></a><span class="italic">Hubei Collaborative Innovation Center for Advanced Organic Chemical Materials, Hubei University, Wuhan 430062, China</span>
<br><a id="affe"><sup><span class="sup_ref italic">e</span></sup></a><span class="italic">Beijing Key Laboratory of Materials Utilization of Nonmetallic Minerals and Solid Wastes, National Laboratory of Mineral Materials, School of Materials Science and Technology, China University of Geosciences, Beijing 100083, China</span>
<br><a id="afff"><sup><span class="sup_ref italic">f</span></sup></a><span class="italic">State Key Laboratory of Solidification Processing, Center for Nano Energy Materials, Northwestern Polytechnical University and Shaanxi Joint Laboratory of Graphene (NPU), Xi'an 710072, China</span>
<br><a id="affg"><sup><span class="sup_ref italic">g</span></sup></a><span class="italic">Department of Materials Science and NanoEngineering, Rice University, Houston, Texas 77005, USA</span>
</p><div id="art-admin"><span class="italic bold">Received 
20th November 2017
</span><span class="bold italic">, Accepted 30th December 2017</span></div><p class="bold italic">First published on 10th January 2018</p><hr><div class="abstract"><h2>Abstract</h2><p>Layered metal sulfides (MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>, WS<small><sub>2</sub></small>, SnS<small><sub>2</sub></small>, and SnS) offer high potential as advanced anode materials in sodium ion batteries upon integration with highly-conductive graphene materials. However, in addition to being costly and time-consuming, existing strategies for synthesizing sulfides/graphene composites often involve complicated procedures. It is therefore essential to develop a simple yet scalable pathway to construct sulfide/graphene composites for practical applications. Here, we highlight a one-step, template-free, high-throughput “self-bubbling” method for producing MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/graphene composites, which is suitable for large-scale production of sulfide/graphene composites. The final product featured MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> nanoflakes distributed in three-dimensional macroporous monolithic graphene. Moreover, this unique MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/graphene composite achieved remarkable electrochemical performance when being applied to Na-ion battery anodes; namely, excellent cycling stability (474 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 0.1 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> after 100 cycles) and high rate capability (406 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 0.25 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> and 359 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 0.5 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>). This self-bubbling approach should be applicable to delivering other graphene-based composites for emerging applications such as energy storage, catalysis, and sensing.</p></div><hr>

<span>Sodium-ion batteries (NIBs) have been proposed as promising alternatives to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) in the megawatt- and kilowatt-scale energy storage scenarios (<span class="italic">i.e.</span>; electric vehicles, stationary grids) for their high cost-effectiveness, sustainability, and environmental benignity.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit1"><sup><span class="sup_ref">1</span></sup></a> Since the operation chemistry of NIBs is very similar to that of LIBs, knowledge gained from developing LIB technology can be mostly applied to NIBs with the exception of electrode materials.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit2"><sup><span class="sup_ref">2,3</span></sup></a> In particular, the larger ionic radius of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> (0.102 nm) than that of Li<small><sup>+</sup></small> (0.076 nm) makes graphite, the most commonly used anode in LIBs, unable to accommodate sodium ions in a satisfactory regime.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit4"><sup><span class="sup_ref">4</span></sup></a> Inspired by the findings on LIBs, scientists have tested carbonaceous materials,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit5"><sup><span class="sup_ref">5,6</span></sup></a> alloy materials (Sn, Sb),<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit7"><sup><span class="sup_ref">7,8</span></sup></a> and metal oxides (Fe<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>3</sub></small>, CuO, TiO<small><sub>2</sub></small>)<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit9"><sup><span class="sup_ref">9–11</span></sup></a> as anode materials for NIBs. Unfortunately, due to the large volume change and/or the sluggish kinetics during charge/discharge cycles, these materials delivered either low reversible capacity or poor cyclability.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit12"><sup><span class="sup_ref">12</span></sup></a> Consequently, layered metal sulfides (MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>, WS<small><sub>2</sub></small>, SnS<small><sub>2</sub></small> and SnS) have also been explored as anode materials in NIBs due to their unique structural characteristics.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit13"><sup><span class="sup_ref">13</span></sup></a> For example, molybdenum sulfide (MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>), stemming from its large interlayer spacing (0.62 nm, compared to 0.34 nm for graphite) and high capacity for hosting foreign species, has been recently highlighted as a possible candidate for anode material in NIBs.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit14"><sup><span class="sup_ref">14–19</span></sup></a> According to the intercalation and conversion reaction between one MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> molecule and four Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>, the theoretical capacity of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> is as high as 670 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit20"><sup><span class="sup_ref">20</span></sup></a></span>
<p class="otherpara">However, there are two major issues when using MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> as anodes in large-scale applications: poor electronic conductivity and drastic volume expansion upon conversion reaction from MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> to Mo and Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>S.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit18"><sup><span class="sup_ref">18,20–27</span></sup></a> One effective approach to address the problems and thus improve the electrochemical performance of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> in NIBs is by supporting MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> with conductive scaffolds to create porous composites, so as to simultaneously improve its conductivity as well as buffer the volumetric variation.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit12"><sup><span class="sup_ref">12</span></sup></a> In this regard, carbon materials, especially graphene, have been repeatedly confirmed to be an efficient conductive additive in electrode materials in resolving the above issues.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit28"><sup><span class="sup_ref">28,29</span></sup></a> Some examples of such effective treatment on electrode materials include sulfur/graphene cathode in lithium–sulfur batteries,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit30"><sup><span class="sup_ref">30</span></sup></a> lithium metal phosphates/carbon cathode materials in LIBs,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit31"><sup><span class="sup_ref">31–34</span></sup></a> and various metal oxides/graphene anode materials in LIBs.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit35"><sup><span class="sup_ref">35</span></sup></a></p>
<p class="otherpara">To improve the electrical conductivity and enhance the structural integrity of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> anode, MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/graphene composites have been synthesized <span class="italic">via</span> several methods and applied in NIBs.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit17"><sup><span class="sup_ref">17,18,21,23,26,36–41</span></sup></a> For instance, David <span class="italic">et al.</span> prepared MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/graphene composite paper through vacuum filtration of homogeneous dispersions consisting of exfoliated MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> and graphene oxide sheets, followed by thermal reduction at elevated temperatures.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit18"><sup><span class="sup_ref">18</span></sup></a> Wang <span class="italic">et al.</span> and Xie <span class="italic">et al.</span> also synthesized MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/graphene composites <span class="italic">via</span> hydrothermal reactions plus thermal annealing, respectively.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit23"><sup><span class="sup_ref">23,26</span></sup></a> In spite of the significant synthetic achievements made, the existing strategies for synthesizing MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/graphene composites present a few shortcomings as these methods often involve complicated procedures (graphene oxide preparation, MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> preparation, compositing or mixing step, thermal treatments, <span class="italic">etc.</span>) in addition to be costly and time-consuming.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit28"><sup><span class="sup_ref">28</span></sup></a> Another issue with existing MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/graphene compositing methods is that some of them do not ensure the intimate contact between MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/graphene interfaces, an unfavorable condition for electrochemical applications (charge-transfer process).<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit26"><sup><span class="sup_ref">26</span></sup></a> Finally, most of the present MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/graphene compositing methods are faced with the issue of low yield, ranging from several tens to hundreds milligrams of powders under laboratory conditions.</p>
<p class="otherpara">Herein, we report a single-step, template-free, high-throughput “self-bubbling” method for synthesizing MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/graphene composite. Our method is cost-effective, simple and scalable. The synthesis utilizes the thermal decomposition of solid precursor to generate MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>; meanwhile, the released gas from the decomposition reaction blows premixed, melted glucose into crowded bubbles, which then evolve into graphene structures during annealing. The final product is microscopically featured as highly crystalline MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> nanoflakes distributed in three-dimensional (3D) macroporous monolithic graphene. With the additional assistance of intimate interfacial contacts between MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> and graphene, our composite demonstrates considerably improved electrochemical performance when compared with those of conventional MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/graphene composite upon application in NIBs. It is expected that such a unique MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/graphene composite should hold potential in promoting the development of practical MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> anode in NIBs, while the straightforward self-bubbling method could offer the opportunity in producing MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/graphene composites in industrial scale as well as synthesizing other advanced graphene-based composites.</p>

<h2 id="sect425"><span class="a_heading">Results and discussion</span></h2>
<span>We demonstrate a one-step “self-bubbling” system, for the first time, to synthesize the graphene/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> composite in this work. Empirically, thermal decomposition of (NH<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>2</sub></small>MoS<small><sub>4</sub></small> in inert atmosphere leads to MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> while releasing a considerable amount of gases.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit42"><sup><span class="sup_ref">42,43</span></sup></a> Results from our carefully conducted thermogravimetric and differential scanning calorimetry (TG/DSC) analysis for (NH<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>2</sub></small>MoS<small><sub>4</sub></small> decomposition in flowing Ar (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1a</a>) suggests the following processes:<table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn1">
<span id="eqn1">(NH<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>2</sub></small>MoS<small><sub>4</sub></small> → 2NH<small><sub>3</sub></small>↑ + H<small><sub>2</sub></small>S↑ + MoS<small><sub>3</sub></small> (210–380 °C)</span>
</span></td><td class="rightEqn">(1)</td></tr></tbody></table><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn2">
<span id="eqn2">MoS<small><sub>3</sub></small> → MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> + S↑ (400–700 °C)</span>
</span></td><td class="rightEqn">(2)</td></tr></tbody></table></span>
<br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig1"><a href="/image/article/2018/RA/c7ra12617d/c7ra12617d-f1_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c7ra12617d-f1.tif" src="/image/article/2018/RA/c7ra12617d/c7ra12617d-f1.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 1 </b> <span id="fig1"><span class="graphic_title">Experimental design of the “self-bubbling” method. (a) TG/DSC profiles of the (NH<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>2</sub></small>MoS<small><sub>4</sub></small> precursor in flowing Ar. (b) The heating program of the “self-bubbling” experiment. (c) The corresponding schematics depicting the synthetic route of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
<p class="otherpara">Inspired by these findings, we started with a mixture of (NH<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>2</sub></small>MoS<small><sub>4</sub></small> (as MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> source) and glucose (as carbon source) with the setup of a two-stage annealing sequence to produce graphene/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> hybrid, as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1b</a>. In the first stage (from room temperature to 200 °C), glucose (m.p. 146 °C) melts into viscous liquid while (NH<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>2</sub></small>MoS<small><sub>4</sub></small> remains solid inside it. Sequentially, during the second stage (from 200 to 1100 °C), (NH<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>2</sub></small>MoS<small><sub>4</sub></small> decomposes into MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> and crystallizes while the released gas species (ammonia, hydrogen sulfide and sulfur vapor) blow the melted glucose to form crowded bubbles with ultrathin walls, which are then graphitized into 3D graphene networks at high temperature (the released gases actually serve as soft templates to direct the growth of graphene structures.). With this simple approach, a rational nanostructure consists of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> and graphene was obtained (hereafter abbreviated as MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid). Besides, our approach also allows the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/graphene ratio in the final product to be expediently tuned by using different ratios of (NH<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>2</sub></small>MoS<small><sub>4</sub></small> and glucose as precursors for various potential applications (see Fig. S1,<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> the composites with varied MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/graphene ratios). The advantages of such novel approach include low cost, high flexibility, easy operability and excellent scalability. The complete annealing program is presented in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1b</a> as well as described in the Experimental methods section in the ESI.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> A schematic diagram of the whole process is further shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1c</a>.</p>
<p class="otherpara">The effectiveness of our approach could be fully confirmed by systematic microstructural analysis of the end-product. Firstly, microscopies were involved to reveal the morphological characteristics of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid. The single production of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid under our laboratory condition is up to <span class="italic">ca.</span> 1–2 g when a 1-inch (diameter) tube furnace was used, and the product is foam-like black solid (inset of <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2a</a>). An optical image (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2a</a>) manifests that the product is composed of large-scale crowded bubbles. The walls of these bubbles are so thin that the light can penetrate through them, leading to rainbow-like reflections on their surface. The scanning electron microscope (SEM) images (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2b and c</a>) suggest the bubbles are mostly polyhedral units, with a broad size distribution from 1 to 50 micrometers in diameter. Enlarged SEM image (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2d</a>) further reveals that the wall of the bubbles is made up of ultrathin nanosheets, and every three to four bubbles are interconnected by a strut (denoted by red arrow). One can also notice the presence of large areas of wrinkle-like structures on the nanosheets (denoted by black arrows), a typical phenomenon associated with large-sized graphene, which helps to further increase the surface area of the sample.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit44"><sup><span class="sup_ref">44</span></sup></a> Such structure of monolithic graphene is analogous to the 3D Voronoi structure (which is frequently seen in soap bubbles and styrofoam)<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit45"><sup><span class="sup_ref">45,46</span></sup></a> and provides a number of advantages such as excellent mechanical stability, high surface area, and effective avoidance of the graphene restack.</p>
<br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig2"><a href="/image/article/2018/RA/c7ra12617d/c7ra12617d-f2_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c7ra12617d-f2.tif" src="/image/article/2018/RA/c7ra12617d/c7ra12617d-f2.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 2 </b> <span id="fig2"><span class="graphic_title">Morphology characterizations of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid. (a) Optical image and digital image (inset) of the product. (b–d) SEM images of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid. (e) Top-view and (f) side-view TEM images of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> nanoflakes distributed on few-layered graphene. (g) SAED pattern of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid. (h and i) HRTEM images of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid. The scale bar in (h) also applies in (i).</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
<p class="otherpara">Our results also show that the graphene walls are decorated with nanosized particulates in the SEM images, most likely the result of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> content. Transmission electron microscope (TEM) images in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2e and f</a> verify the nanoparticles are quasi-hexagonal nanoflakes (in consistent with our previous <span class="italic">in situ</span> experiment<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit22"><sup><span class="sup_ref">22</span></sup></a>), with a lateral size of 50–100 nm and thickness of 5–10 nm. As expected, the selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2g</a> can be readily assigned to hexagonal MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> structure (JCPDS no. 37-1492) while the bright diffraction rings reflect high crystallinity (also refer to Fig. S2,<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> the energy dispersive spectra (EDS) from the nanoflakes region). The diffraction rings in the SAED pattern correspond to a polycrystalline character, a result of the cumulative signals from many nanoflakes across the selected area aperture (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2e</a>) although the high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) image in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2h</a> shows that each MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> nanoflake is clearly a single crystal. The intimate contact between MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> nanoflakes and graphene nanosheets is also evident from the TEM images (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2e–i</a>), a favorable condition for enhancing the electroactivity of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit26"><sup><span class="sup_ref">26</span></sup></a> Interestingly, despite the validation of the in-plane <span class="italic">d</span>-spacing of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> (0.26 nm for (100) planes) and interlayer distance of graphene (0.34 nm for (002) planes) as shown in the HRTEM images (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2h and i</a>), a slightly expanded interlayer distance of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> (∼0.65 nm, 0.62 nm for natural MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>) can be identified throughout repeated observations. It should be noted that MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> structure with expanded interlayers is commonly considered to be highly beneficial to improve its electrochemical performance (discharge capacity, reaction kinetics, <span class="italic">etc.</span>) for battery applications.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit20"><sup><span class="sup_ref">20,27</span></sup></a> In short, the above results consistently showed that the sample from our one-step self-bubbling approach was MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> nanoflakes distributed in macroporous few-layered graphene, in accordance with our original design. For comparison purpose, we also prepared pure MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> samples <span class="italic">via</span> the same process without glucose and the final product is mainly irregular microsized flakes (see Fig. S3,<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> the TEM images of pure MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> sample).</p>
<p class="otherpara">Subsequently, spectroscopic characterizations were employed to further explore the microstructural features of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid. Concerning the chemical states of Mo and S in the product, <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3a</a> shows the X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) survey scans for MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> and MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid, with their C 1s peak referenced at 284.8 eV. The presence of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> with Mo and S elemental ratio of ∼1<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">2 can be identified for both samples, besides the prominent carbon component in the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid. The insets in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3a</a> show the high-resolution spectra of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid, which are the S 2p, Mo 3d and C 1s regions. The Mo 3d possesses two peaks centered at 229.6 and 232.8 eV, in association with the doublet Mo 3d<small><sub>5/2</sub></small> and Mo 3d<small><sub>3/2</sub></small> for Mo<small><sup>4+</sup></small> ions. Another group of peaks, ascribed to the S 2p<small><sub>3/2</sub></small> and S 2p<small><sub>1/2</sub></small> orbital of divalent sulfide ions (S<small><sup>2−</sup></small>), are observed at 162.4 and 163.7 eV, respectively. All these results are well consistent with the reported values for MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit25"><sup><span class="sup_ref">25,47</span></sup></a> The existence of Mo, S and C in the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid was also verified by the electron energy-loss spectrum (EELS), as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3b</a> by the S L-edge, Mo M-edge and C K-edge. Particularly, the core-loss C K-edge EELS spectrum of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid (inset in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3b</a>) presents a sharp π* peak (∼284 eV, due to the excitation from 1s spin level to empty π* orbits of the sp<small><sup>2</sup></small>-bonded atoms) as well as a clear σ* step (∼289 eV, resulting from the transition from the 1s level to empty σ* orbits at both sp<small><sup>2</sup></small> and sp<small><sup>3</sup></small>-bonded atoms), suggesting the high crystalline nature of graphene in the hybrid.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit30"><sup><span class="sup_ref">30,48</span></sup></a></p>
<br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig3"><a href="/image/article/2018/RA/c7ra12617d/c7ra12617d-f3_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c7ra12617d-f3.tif" src="/image/article/2018/RA/c7ra12617d/c7ra12617d-f3.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 3 </b> <span id="fig3"><span class="graphic_title">Microstructural analysis of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid and the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>. (a) XPS spectra of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid and MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>, with high-resolution S 2p, Mo 3d, and C 1s spectra for the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid as the insets. (b) EELS spectrum and the core-loss C K-edge spectrum (inset) of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid. (c) Comparison of the XRD patterns of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid and MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>. (d) Raman spectra of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid and MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> in MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> region and graphene region (inset). (e) Thermogravimetric profiles for MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid and MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>, obtained by annealing the samples in synthetic air. (f) N<small><sub>2</sub></small> adsorption/desorption isotherms of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid and the corresponding pore-size distribution (inset).</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
<p class="otherpara">The crystal structure of the samples was then studied by X-ray diffraction (XRD). As shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3c</a>, the XRD patterns for both MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> and MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid match with 2H molybdenite; however, the diffraction peaks of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid are much boarder than those of pure MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>, a result of the fine MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> crystalline size. Notably, the peak of (002) planes for the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid slightly shifts towards the direction of low scattering angle, corroborating the expanded interlayer distance as revealed by the above TEM results. The diffraction signal for graphene is not visible due to the intense peaks from MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> crystals, so Raman measurement was applied. As can be seen from the inset of <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3d</a>, the Raman spectrum for MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid exhibits two sharp bands at 1360 (D band, in-plane vibration of sp<small><sup>3</sup></small>-bonded carbon) and 1600 cm<small><sup>−1</sup></small> (G band, vibration mode of sp<small><sup>2</sup></small>-bonded carbon), as well as two broad bands at 2690 (2D band) and 2920 cm<small><sup>−1</sup></small> (D + G band),<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit49"><sup><span class="sup_ref">49</span></sup></a> which directly proved the existence of well-crystallized few-layered graphene structure in the hybrid. The hexagonal layered structure of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> in the hybrid was further confirmed by Raman spectrum with two peaks located at 383 and 407 cm<small><sup>−1</sup></small> (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3d</a>), which are typical E<small><sup>1</sup></small><small><sub>2g</sub></small> and A<small><sub>1g</sub></small> modes due to in-plane vibrations within the sulfur–molybdenum–sulfur layers, respectively.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit50"><sup><span class="sup_ref">50</span></sup></a> Consequently, the carbon content in the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid was measured by annealing the sample in synthetic air upon TG/DSC test. Assuming the complete formation of MoO<small><sub>3</sub></small>, SO<small><sub>2</sub></small> and CO<small><sub>2</sub></small>,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit51"><sup><span class="sup_ref">51</span></sup></a> the graphene content is estimated to be 27.8 wt%, corresponding to the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> content of 72.2 wt% (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3e</a>).</p>
<p class="otherpara">To further characterize the composite structure, the specific surface area and porous nature of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid was quantified by Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) method. Results from the full nitrogen adsorption and desorption isotherms (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3f</a>) present typical type-IV characteristics with type-H4 hysteresis loop at a relative pressure above 0.5, indicating a nanoporous structure. Accordingly, the surface area of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid is as high as 196.93 m<small><sup>2</sup></small> g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>; in contrast, the surface area of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> sample is 6.22 m<small><sup>2</sup></small> g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> (Fig. S4<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>). It is also worth noting that the pore sizes of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid, derived <span class="italic">via</span> the Barrett–Joyner–Halenda (BJH) method, are mainly distributed in the region of mesopores to macropores with a peak centered at 3.87 nm (inset in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3f</a>). The high surface area of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid together with the ample pores would be extremely favorable for energy storage applications such as batteries. The porosity in the Voronoi-structured framework can act as efficient electrolyte reservoirs to enlarge the contact areas between electrolyte and the active materials, and increase the active sites for sodiation/desodiation. Meanwhile, the porosity can also buffer the volume change to avoid structural pulverization during repeated charge/discharge cycles.</p>
<p class="otherpara">The above structural characterizations of our MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid suggest the high potential of applying the product as electrode materials in NIBs. To verify this, systematic electrochemical measurements were performed with CR2032 coin cells. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4a</a> shows the cyclic voltammograms (CVs) of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid during the initial five cycles in the potential range of 0.01–3 V <span class="italic">versus</span> Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na. In the first cathodic scan (sodiation), the first peak at 1.20 V can be ascribed to the intercalation of sodium ions into MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> interlayer (refer to Fig. S5<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> for the isolated first scan).<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit24"><sup><span class="sup_ref">24</span></sup></a> The following two reduction peaks at 0.66 and 0.56 V are attributed to the two-step insertion of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> into MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit26"><sup><span class="sup_ref">26,52</span></sup></a> The fourth subtle peak located at ∼0.35 V is related to the conversion reaction from MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> to Mo and Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>S.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit18"><sup><span class="sup_ref">18</span></sup></a> The last sharp cathodic peak at 0.02 V is associated with the intercalation of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> into the graphene interlayers.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit53"><sup><span class="sup_ref">53</span></sup></a> In the subsequent anodic scan (desodiation), the peaks from 1.4 to 1.7 V should be attributed to the oxidation of Mo to MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit27"><sup><span class="sup_ref">27</span></sup></a> In the subsequent cycles, the peaks at 0.66/0.56 V shift to 1.04/0.75 V with decreased intensity, corresponding to the progressive amorphization of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid. Notably, the CVs rapidly become overlapped in the later cycles, suggesting high reversibility for the electrode material. The stability of sodiation/desodiation processes was also confirmed by comparing the cycling performances of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid and MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> under the galvanostatic mode at a current density of 0.1 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4b</a>. First, both samples show initial capacity drops as well as low coulombic efficiencies (CE) at the first cycle, which should be a result of the formation of solid electrolyte interface (SEI) film. Second, the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid delivered a capacity as high as 484 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at the 2nd cycle and 474 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at the 100th cycle (corresponding to a small capacity decay of 0.02% per cycle). In contrast, the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> electrode delivered only a capacity of 268 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at the 2nd cycle and 97 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at the 50th cycle. Furthermore, the CE of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid (&gt;99%) is constantly higher than that of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>.</p>
<br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig4"><a href="/image/article/2018/RA/c7ra12617d/c7ra12617d-f4_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c7ra12617d-f4.tif" src="/image/article/2018/RA/c7ra12617d/c7ra12617d-f4.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 4 </b> <span id="fig4"><span class="graphic_title">Electrochemical performances of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid and the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> as anode materials in NIBs. (a) The initial CV profiles of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid measured at 0.2 mV s<small><sup>−1</sup></small> in the voltage window of 0.01–3 V. (b) Comparison of cycling performances of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid and MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> at 0.1 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> and the schematic of transport paths for Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> and electrons in the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid (inset). (c) The initial galvanostatic discharge/charge curves for MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid at 0.05 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> in the voltage range of 0.01–3 V. (d) Rate performance of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid at programmed current densities. (e) Comparison of the long-term cycling behaviors of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid and MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> at 0.5 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
<p class="otherpara"><a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4c</a> displays the galvanostatic discharge/charge voltage profiles during the first five cycles of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid at 0.05 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> in the potential window of 0.01–3 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na. The distinct discharge/charge plateaus in the first cycle are ascribed to the sample's high crystallinity.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit23"><sup><span class="sup_ref">23</span></sup></a> The discharge and charge capacities for the first cycle are 750 and 630 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, respectively, corresponding to a CE of 83.7% (in line with the low initial CE in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4b</a>). In the subsequent cycles, the discharge and charge profiles become identical, and no obvious discharge/charge plateau can be identified. These cells were then involved in the test of rate capability and the result is presented in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4d</a>. The specific discharge capacities are 530, 475, 408, and 357 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 0.05, 0.1, 0.25, and 0.5 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, respectively; <span class="italic">i.e.</span>, when the current density is increased by ten times (from 0.05 to 0.5 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>), the electrode material can still retain ∼67% of its capacity (from 530 to 357 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>). Moreover, the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid is able to recover most of its original capacity when the current rate is restored back to 0.05 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> after forty deep cycles, indicating the high stability of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G anode even upon high rate cycling (also refer to Fig. S6–S8<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> for the CV profiles, discharge/charge curves, and rate performance of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> sample, as well as Fig. S9<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> for the cycling performance of graphene). Therefore, another cycling test was conducted under the galvanostatic mode at a higher current density (0.5 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4e</a>). After 200 cycles, the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G anode remarkably preserves its sodium storage capacity at as high as 371 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> whereas the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> anode almost loses its electrochemical activity (final capacity of 31 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>). It is worth noting that, during these repeated discharge/charge cycles, the volume change induced pulverization of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid electrode has been significantly suppressed, as reflected by the constantly high CE (&gt;99%) throughout the measurement and the comparative postmortem TEM study. As shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5</a>, the postmortem TEM study of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid indicates the existence of small MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> flakes firmly decorated on graphene sheets. The MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> content remains highly crystallized except the irregular outlines after such long cycling. Moreover, the uniform distribution of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> on graphene sheets was also successful maintained. In contrast, the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> sample shows considerable cracks across the flakes throughout the cycling (Fig. S10<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>).</p>
<br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig5"><a href="/image/article/2018/RA/c7ra12617d/c7ra12617d-f5_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c7ra12617d-f5.tif" src="/image/article/2018/RA/c7ra12617d/c7ra12617d-f5.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 5 </b> <span id="fig5"><span class="graphic_title">TEM characterizations for MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid after cycling. (a) TEM image and the corresponding SAED pattern (inset); (b) magnified high-resolution TEM image (the discharged products and electrolyte residuals are visible as irregular particles); (c) the STEM (scanning transmission electron microscopy) image of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid and the corresponding (d) C, (e) Mo, and (f) S maps from the region indicated by the red rectangle in (c).</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
<p class="otherpara">The above electrochemical characterizations substantiate the fact that our MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid produced by the novel “self-bubbling” approach possesses high reversible capacity, excellent rate capability, as well as superior cycling stability as anode material in NIBs. Such impressive performance, to the best of our knowledge, is among one of the best values for MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-based anode materials for NIBs (refer to Table S1,<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> the comparison of electrochemical performances with selected MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-based anode materials for NIBs). We believe that the unique microstructural features of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid itself have brought multiple advantages to act as high-performance electrode materials. First, the thin MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> nanoflakes (5–10 nm in thickness) with expanded interlayer spacing (0.65 nm) greatly reduces the strain caused by insertion and extraction of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>. Second, the high surface area of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid (196.93 m<small><sup>2</sup></small> g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>) provides a large electrode–electrolyte interface, in facilitating the critical charge-transfer process. Third, the monolithic graphene ensures a 3D porous and flexible scaffold to protect MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> from dissolution or detachment during repeated cycles, as well as 3D interconnected pathways for electron transport and Na-ion diffusion. Finally, the homogeneous distribution of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> nanoflakes on graphene nanosheets together with their intimate contact guarantees good conductivity (for both Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> and electrons), and hence a high level of electrochemical activity and effective material utilization of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> (as shown in the inset of <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4b</a>).</p>

<h2 id="sect719"><span class="a_heading">Conclusions</span></h2>
<span>In summary, we have developed a simple and scalable “self-bubbling” approach for synthesizing an advanced MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid composed of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> nanoflakes entrapped in 3D macroporous monolithic graphene frameworks. Benefiting from the unique microstructural characteristics, the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid has shown outstanding electrochemical performance including remarkable cycling stability and high rate capability upon used as anode material for NIBs. On account of the promising structural tunability of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid, we also believe that the product might possess great potential in other application areas such as supercapacitors, catalysts, and sensors. Our one-step method may be also applicable in constructing other emerging graphene-based composites, and should therefore inspire further attempts to additional application scenes in future.</span>

<h2 id="sect726"><span class="a_heading">Experimental methods</span></h2>
<h3 id="sect728"><span class="b_heading">Synthesis of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid</span></h3>
<span>The MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>@G hybrid was synthesized from a novel “self-bubbling” approach. A mixed powder of (NH<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>2</sub></small>MoS<small><sub>4</sub></small> and glucose (weight ratio of 1<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">1.5) was directly subjected to thermal treatment in a horizontal furnace. As shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1b</a>, the temperature was first ramped from room temperature to 200 °C in 40 minutes and this temperature was kept for 60 minutes. The temperature was then further increased to 1100 °C in 180 minutes and hold for another 180 minutes. The product was harvested after cooling the system to room temperature in 110 minutes. The whole annealing process was protected by a constant argon flow.</span>

<h3 id="sect737"><span class="b_heading">Material characterizations</span></h3>
<span>The samples were characterized by different analytical techniques. Simultaneous TG/DSC analysis was performed on a NETZSCH STA 449 C Jupiter system. Optical image was captured on a Nikon Microphot-FXA microscope. SEM observations were made on a JEOL JSM-6700F field-emission SEM. TEM images, SAED pattern, EELS, and EDS were obtained on a JEOL JEM-2100F STEM (200 kV, field-emission gun) system equipped with an Oxford INCA x-sight EDS and an ENFINA 1000 EELS. XPS spectra were acquired on a Thermo Scientific Escalab 250Xi spectrometer. XRD measurement was conducted using a Rigaku SmartLab Intelligent X-ray diffraction system with filtered Cu K<small><sub>α</sub></small> radiation (<span class="italic">λ</span> = 1.5406 Å, operating at 45 kV and 200 mA). Raman measurement was taken using a Horiba Jobin Yvon LabRAM HR system with a laser wavelength of 488 nm. The nitrogen adsorption and desorption isotherms were obtained at 77 K with a Micromeritics ASAP 2020 volumetric adsorption analyzer.</span>

<h3 id="sect742"><span class="b_heading">Electrochemical measurements</span></h3>
<span>The working electrode slurry was prepared by mixing the active materials with Super P and carboxymethyl cellulose binder at a mass ratio of 8<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">1<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">1. The slurry was then spread on the surface of a copper foil and dried at 60 °C for 12 h. Finally, the electrode was stamped into disks with a diameter of 10 mm and vacuum-dried at 60 °C for another 6 h. With sodium tablets as the reference electrode and glass fiber membrane as the separator, CR2032 coin cells were assembled in a glove box (MIKROUNA-Universal-2440-1750) filled with argon. 1 M NaClO<small><sub>4</sub></small> in the mixed solvent of ethylene carbonate/dimethyl carbonate (1<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">1 v/v ratio) with 5 wt% fluoroethylene carbonate as additive was selected as the electrolyte for the coin cells. CV measurement was conducted on an electrochemical measurement system (PARSTAT 2273) with a scan rate of 0.2 mV s<small><sup>−1</sup></small> from 3 to 0.01 V. Galvanostatic charge/discharge tests were performed by using a battery testing system (LAND CT2001A) within the potential of 0.01–3 V at room temperature. The capacities are given with respect to the total mass of the active materials throughout the work.</span>
